Armstrong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Collins_(astronaut)" title="Michael Collins (astronaut)">Michael Collins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Buzz Aldrin</a><div class="" style="width:100%; text-align:center; border-top:1px #aaa solid"><a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a></div><div style="width:100%"><div class="noprint" style="float: left; text-align:left;padding-right:0.5em;">&#8592;&#160;<a href="/wiki/Apollo_10" title="Apollo 10">Apollo 10</a></div><div class="noprint" style="float: right; text-align:right;padding-left:0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Apollo_12" title="Apollo 12">Apollo 12</a>&#160;&#8594;</div></div>&#160;</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Apollo 11</b> was the <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">spaceflight</a> that <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing" title="Moon landing">landed</a> the first two people on the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.  Commander <a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">Neil Armstrong</a> and lunar module pilot <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Buzz Aldrin</a>, both American, landed the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">Apollo Lunar Module</a> <i>Eagle</i> on July 20, 1969, at 20:17 <a href="/wiki/Coordinated_Universal_Time" title="Coordinated Universal Time">UTC</a>. Armstrong became the first person to step onto the lunar surface six hours later on July 21 at 02:56:15 UTC; Aldrin joined him 19 minutes later. They spent about two and a quarter hours together outside the spacecraft, and collected 47.5 pounds (21.5&#160;kg) of lunar material to bring back to Earth. Command module pilot <a href="/wiki/Michael_Collins_(astronaut)" title="Michael Collins (astronaut)">Michael Collins</a> flew the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_command_and_service_module" title="Apollo command and service module">command module</a> <i>Columbia</i> alone in lunar orbit while they were on the Moon\'s surface. Armstrong and Aldrin spent 21.5 hours on the lunar surface before rejoining <i>Columbia</i> in lunar orbit.\n</p><p>Apollo 11 was launched by a <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a> rocket from <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a> on <a href="/wiki/Merritt_Island,_Florida" title="Merritt Island, Florida">Merritt Island, Florida</a>, on July 16 at 13:32 UTC, and was the fifth crewed mission of <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a>. The Apollo <a href="/wiki/Apollo_(spacecraft)" title="Apollo (spacecraft)">spacecraft</a> had three parts: a command module (CM) with a cabin for the three astronauts, and the only part that returned to Earth; a service module (SM), which supported the command module with propulsion, electrical power, oxygen, and water; and a lunar module (LM) that had two stages &#8211; a descent stage for landing on the Moon, and an ascent stage to place the astronauts back into lunar orbit.\n</p><p>After being <a href="/wiki/Trans-lunar_injection" title="Trans-lunar injection">sent to the Moon</a> by the Saturn V\'s third stage, the astronauts separated the spacecraft from it and traveled for three days until they entered lunar orbit. Armstrong and Aldrin then moved into <i>Eagle</i> and landed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_of_Tranquillity" title="Sea of Tranquillity">Sea of Tranquillity</a>. The astronauts used <i>Eagle</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s ascent stage to lift off from the lunar surface and rejoin Collins in the command module. They jettisoned <i>Eagle</i> before they performed the maneuvers that blasted them out of lunar orbit on a trajectory back to Earth. They returned to Earth and <a href="/wiki/Splashdown" title="Splashdown">splashed down</a> in the Pacific Ocean on July 24 after more than eight days in space.\n</p><p>Armstrong\'s first step onto the lunar surface was broadcast on live TV to a worldwide audience. He described the event as "one small step for [a] man, one giant leap for mankind."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALSJ_4_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-ALSJ_4-7">[7]</a></sup> Apollo 11 effectively ended the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a> and fulfilled a national goal proposed in 1961 by <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>: "before this decade is out, of landing a man on the Moon and returning him safely to the Earth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Mission</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Launch_and_flight_to_lunar_orbit"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span tocsection-19"><a href="#Lunar_surface_operations"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Lunar surface operations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Lunar_ascent"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Lunar ascent</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Columbia_in_lunar_orbit"><span class="tocnumber">4.6</span> <span class="toctext"><i>Columbia</i> in lunar orbit</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Return"><span class="tocnumber">4.7</span> <span quarantine</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Celebrations"><span class="tocnumber">4.9</span> <span class="toctext">Celebrations</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Legacy"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Cultural_significance"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Cultural significance</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Spacecraft"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Spacecraft</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Moon_rocks"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Moon class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#50th_Anniversary_events"><span class="tocnumber">5.5</span> <span class="toctext">50th Anniversary events</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#NASA_reports"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">NASA reports</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-35"><a href="#Multimedia"><span class="tocnumber">8.2</span> <span class="toctext">Multimedia</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h2>\n<p>In the late 1950s and early 1960s, the United States was engaged in the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, a geopolitical rivalry with the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELogsdon1976134_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELogsdon1976134-9">[9]</a></sup> On October 4, 1957, the Soviet Union launched <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Sputnik 1</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">artificial satellite</a>. This surprise success fired fears and imaginations around the world. It demonstrated that the Soviet Union had the capability to deliver nuclear weapons over intercontinental distances, and challenged American claims of military, economic and technological superiority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELogsdon197613&#8211;15_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELogsdon197613&#8211;15-10">[10]</a></sup> This precipitated the <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_crisis" title="Sputnik crisis">Sputnik crisis</a>, and triggered the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson19791_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson19791-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> responded to the Sputnik challenge by creating the  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration" title="National Aeronautics and Space Administration">National Aeronautics and Space Administration</a> (NASA), and initiating <a href="/wiki/Project_Mercury" title="Project Mercury">Project Mercury</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwensonGrimwoodAlexander1966101&#8211;106_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwensonGrimwoodAlexander1966101&#8211;106-12">[12]</a></sup> which aimed to launch a man into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_orbit" title="Earth orbit">Earth orbit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwensonGrimwoodAlexander1966134_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwensonGrimwoodAlexander1966134-13">[13]</a></sup> But on April 12, 1961, Soviet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmonaut" title="Cosmonaut">cosmonaut</a> <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a> became the first person in space, and the first to orbit the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwensonGrimwoodAlexander1966332&#8211;333_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwensonGrimwoodAlexander1966332&#8211;333-14">[14]</a></sup> It was another body blow to American pride.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwensonGrimwoodAlexander1966342_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwensonGrimwoodAlexander1966342-15">[15]</a></sup> Nearly a month later, on May 5, 1961, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">Alan Shepard</a> became the first American in space, completing a 15-minute suborbital journey. After being recovered from the Atlantic Ocean, he received a congratulatory telephone call from Eisenhower\'s successor, <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELogsdon1976121_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELogsdon1976121-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nKennedy believed that it was in the national interest of the United States to be superior to other nations, and that the perception of American power was at least as important as the actuality. It was therefore intolerable that the Soviet Union was more advanced in the field of space exploration. He was determined that the United States should compete, and sought a challenge that maximized its chances of winning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELogsdon1976134_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELogsdon1976134-9">[9]</a></sup> Since the Soviet Union had better <a href="/wiki/Booster_(rocketry)" title="Booster (rocketry)">booster rockets</a>, he required a challenge that was beyond the capacity of the existing generation of rocketry, one where the US and Soviet Union would be starting from a position of equality. Something spectacular, even if it could not be justified on military, economic or scientific grounds. After consulting with his experts and advisors, he chose such a project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELogsdon1976112&#8211;117_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELogsdon1976112&#8211;117-17">[17]</a></sup> On May 25, 1961, he addressed the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> on "Urgent National Needs" and declared:<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I believe that this nation should commit itself to achieving the goal, before this decade is out, of landing a man on the moon and returning him safely to the Earth. No single space project in this period will be more impressive to mankind, or more important for the long-range exploration of space; and none will be so difficult or expensive to accomplish. We propose to accelerate the development of the appropriate lunar space craft. We propose to develop alternate liquid and solid fuel boosters, much larger than any now being developed, until certain which is superior. We propose additional funds for other engine development and for unmanned explorations-explorations which are particularly important for one purpose which this nation will never overlook: the survival of the man who first makes this daring flight. But in a very real sense, it will not be one man going to the Moon-if we make this judgment affirmatively, it will be an entire nation. For all of us must work to put him there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The effort to land a man on the Moon already had a name: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Project_Apollo" title="Project Apollo">Project Apollo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson197915_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson197915-19">[19]</a></sup> An early and crucial decision was choosing <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit_rendezvous" title="Lunar orbit rendezvous">lunar orbit rendezvous</a> over both <a href="/wiki/Direct_ascent" title="Direct ascent">direct ascent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Earth_orbit_rendezvous" title="Earth orbit rendezvous">Earth orbit rendezvous</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Space_rendezvous" title="Space rendezvous">space rendezvous</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Orbital_maneuver" title="Orbital maneuver">orbital maneuver</a> in which two spacecraft navigate through space and meet up. On July 11, 1962, James Webb announced the decision to use lunar orbit rendezvous. This resulted in a much smaller launch vehicle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwensonGrimwoodAlexander196685&#8211;86_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwensonGrimwoodAlexander196685&#8211;86-21">[21]</a></sup> and in the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_(spacecraft)" title="Apollo (spacecraft)">Apollo spacecraft</a> being composed of three major parts: a command module (CM) with a cabin for the three astronauts, and the only part that returned to Earth; a service module (SM), which supported the command module with propulsion, electrical power, oxygen, and water; and a lunar module (LM) that had two stages &#8211; a descent stage for landing on the Moon, and an ascent stage to place the astronauts back into lunar orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson197972&#8211;77_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson197972&#8211;77-22">[22]</a></sup> This choice of mode meant that the spacecraft could be launched by a single <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a> rocket that was then under development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson197948&#8211;49_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson197948&#8211;49-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Technologies and technics required for Apollo were developed by <a href="/wiki/Project_Gemini" title="Project Gemini">Project Gemini</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979181&#8211;182,_205&#8211;208_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979181&#8211;182,_205&#8211;208-24">[24]</a></sup> Project Apollo was abruptly halted by the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_1" title="Apollo 1">Apollo 1</a> fire on January 27, 1967, in which three astronauts died, and the subsequent investigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979214&#8211;218_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979214&#8211;218-25">[25]</a></sup> In October 1968, <a href="/wiki/Apollo_7" title="Apollo 7">Apollo 7</a> evaluated the command module in Earth orbit,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979265&#8211;272_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979265&#8211;272-26">[26]</a></sup> and in December <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">Apollo 8</a> tested it in lunar orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979274&#8211;284_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979274&#8211;284-27">[27]</a></sup> In March 1969, <a href="/wiki/Apollo_9" title="Apollo 9">Apollo 9</a> put the lunar module through its paces in Earth orbit,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979292&#8211;300_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979292&#8211;300-28">[28]</a></sup> and in May <a href="/wiki/Apollo_10" title="Apollo 10">Apollo 10</a> conducted a "dress rehearsal" in lunar orbit. By July 1969, all was in readiness for Apollo 11 to take the final step onto the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979303&#8211;312_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979303&#8211;312-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Soviet Union competed with the US in the Space Race, but its early lead was lost through repeated failures in development of the <a href="/wiki/N1_(rocket)" title="N1 (rocket)">N1 launcher</a>, which was comparable to the Saturn V.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The Soviets tried to beat the US to return lunar material to the Earth by means of <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_spacecraft" title="Unmanned spacecraft">unmanned probes</a>. On July 13, three days before Apollo 11\'s launch, the Soviet Union launched <a href="/wiki/Luna_15" title="Luna 15">Luna 15</a>, which reached lunar orbit before Apollo 11. During descent, a malfunction caused Luna 15 to crash in <a href="/wiki/Mare_Crisium" title="Mare Crisium">Mare Crisium</a> about two hours before Armstrong and Aldrin took off from the Moon\'s surface to begin their voyage home. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuffield_Radio_Astronomy_Laboratories" title="Nuffield Radio Astronomy Laboratories">Nuffield Radio Astronomy Laboratories</a> radio telescope in England recorded transmissions from Luna 15 during its descent, and these were released in July 2009 for the 40th anniversary of Apollo 11.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel">Personnel</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prime_crew">Prime crew</span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable plainrowheaders">\n<caption>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th style="width:150px;"><a href="/wiki/Astronaut_ranks_and_positions" title="Astronaut ranks and positions">Position</a>\n</th>\n<th colspan="2" scope="col" style="width:200px;">Astronaut\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Commander\n</th>\n<td colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">Neil A. Armstrong</a><br/>Second and last spaceflight\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Command Module Pilot\n</th>\n<td colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Michael_Collins_(astronaut)" title="Michael Collins (astronaut)">Michael Collins</a><br/>Second and last spaceflight\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Lunar Module Pilot\n</th>\n<td colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Edwin "Buzz" E. Aldrin Jr.</a><br/>Second and last spaceflight\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<p>The initial crew assignment of Commander <a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">Neil Armstrong</a>, Command Module Pilot (CMP) <a href="/wiki/Jim_Lovell" title="Jim Lovell">Jim Lovell</a>, and Lunar Module Pilot (LMP) <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Buzz Aldrin</a> on the backup crew for Apollo 9 was officially announced on November 20, 1967.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979374_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979374-32">[32]</a></sup> Lovell and Aldrin had previously flown together as the crew of <a href="/wiki/Gemini_12" title="Gemini 12">Gemini 12</a>. Due to design and manufacturing delays in the LM, Apollo 8 and Apollo 9 swapped prime and backup crews, and Armstrong\'s crew became the backup for Apollo 8. Based on the normal crew rotation scheme, Armstrong was then expected to command Apollo 11.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2005312&#8211;313_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2005312&#8211;313-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There would be one change. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Collins_(astronaut)" title="Michael Collins (astronaut)">Michael Collins</a>, the CMP on the Apollo 8 crew, began experiencing trouble with his legs. Doctors diagnosed the problem as a bony growth between his fifth and sixth vertebrae, requiring surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001288&#8211;289_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001288&#8211;289-34">[34]</a></sup> Lovell took his place on the Apollo 8 crew, and when Collins recovered he joined Armstrong\'s crew as CMP. In the meantime, <a href="/wiki/Fred_Haise" title="Fred Haise">Fred Haise</a> filled in as backup LMP, and Aldrin as backup CMP for Apollo 8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunningham2010109_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunningham2010109-35">[35]</a></sup> Apollo 11 was the second American mission where all the crewmembers had prior spaceflight experience,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrloff200090_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrloff200090-36">[36]</a></sup> the first being Apollo 10.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrloff200072_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrloff200072-37">[37]</a></sup> The next was <a href="/wiki/STS-26" title="STS-26">STS-26</a> in 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrloff200090_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrloff200090-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Slayton gave Armstrong the option to replace Aldrin with Lovell, since some thought Aldrin was difficult to work with. Armstrong had no issues working with Aldrin, but thought it over for a day before declining. He thought Lovell deserved to command his own mission (eventually <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13" title="Apollo 13">Apollo 13</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2005338&#8211;339_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2005338&#8211;339-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Apollo 11 prime crew had none of the close cheerful camaraderie that characterized that of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_12" title="Apollo 12">Apollo 12</a>. Instead they forged an amiable working relationship. Armstrong in particular was notoriously aloof, but Collins, who considered himself a loner, confessed to rebuffing Aldrin\'s attempts to create a more personal relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001434&#8211;435_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001434&#8211;435-39">[39]</a></sup> Aldrin and Collins described the crew as "amiable strangers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2005359_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2005359-40">[40]</a></sup> Armstrong did not agree with the assessment, and said "...all the crews I was on worked very well together."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2005359_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2005359-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Backup_crew">Backup crew</span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable plainrowheaders">\n<caption>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th style="width:150px;"><a href="/wiki/Astronaut_ranks_and_positions" title="Astronaut ranks and positions">Position</a>\n</th>\n<th colspan="2" scope="col" style="width:200px;">Astronaut\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Commander\n</th>\n<td colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Jim_Lovell" title="Jim Lovell">James A. Lovell Jr.</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Command Module Pilot\n</th>\n<td colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/William_Anders" title="William Anders">William A. Anders</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Lunar Module Pilot\n</th>\n<td colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Fred_Haise" title="Fred Haise">Fred W. Haise Jr.</a>\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<p>The backup crew consisted of Lovell as Commander, <a href="/wiki/William_Anders" title="William Anders">William Anders</a> as CMP, and Haise as LMP. Anders had flown with Lovell on Apollo 8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrloff200090_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrloff200090-36">[36]</a></sup> In early 1969, he accepted a job with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Council" title="National Aeronautics and Space Council">National Aeronautics and Space Council</a> effective August 1969, and announced that he would retire as an astronaut at that time. <a href="/wiki/Ken_Mattingly" title="Ken Mattingly">Ken Mattingly</a> was moved from the support crew into parallel training with Anders as backup CMP in case Apollo 11 was delayed past its intended July launch date, at which point Anders would be unavailable. Lovell, Haise, and Mattingly were later assigned as the prime crew of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13" title="Apollo 13">Apollo 13</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESlaytonCassutt1994237_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESlaytonCassutt1994237-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Support_crew">Support crew</span></h3>\n<p>During Projects Mercury and Gemini, each mission had a prime and a backup crew. For Apollo, a third crew of astronauts was added, known as the support crew. The support crew maintained the flight plan, checklists and mission ground rules, and ensured that the prime and backup crews were apprised of changes. They developed procedures, especially those for emergency situations, so these were ready for when the prime and backup crews came to train in the simulators, allowing them to concentrate on practicing and mastering them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979261_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979261-42">[42]</a></sup> For Apollo 11, the support crew consisted of Ken Mattingly, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Evans_(astronaut)" title="Ronald Evans (astronaut)">Ronald Evans</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_R._Pogue" title="William R. Pogue">Bill Pogue</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979375_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979375-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capsule_communicators">Capsule communicators</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Duke,_Lovell_and_Haise_at_the_Apollo_11_Capcom,_Johnson_Space_Center,_Houston,_Texas_-_19690720.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2784" data-file-width="4132" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Duke%2C_Lovell_and_Haise_at_the_Apollo_11_Capcom%2C_Johnson_Space_Center%2C_Houston%2C_Texas_-_19690720.jpg/220px-Duke%2C_Lovell_and_Haise_at_the_Apollo_11_Capcom%2C_Johnson_Space_Center%2C_Houston%2C_Texas_-_19690720.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Duke%2C_Lovell_and_Haise_at_the_Apollo_11_Capcom%2C_Johnson_Space_Center%2C_Houston%2C_Texas_-_19690720.jpg/330px-Duke%2C_Lovell_and_Haise_at_the_Apollo_11_Capcom%2C_Johnson_Space_Center%2C_Houston%2C_Texas_-_19690720.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Duke%2C_Lovell_and_Haise_at_the_Apollo_11_Capcom%2C_Johnson_Space_Center%2C_Houston%2C_Texas_-_19690720.jpg/440px-Duke%2C_Lovell_and_Haise_at_the_Apollo_11_Capcom%2C_Johnson_Space_Center%2C_Houston%2C_Texas_-_19690720.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Duke,_Lovell_and_Haise_at_the_Apollo_11_Capcom,_Johnson_Space_Center,_Houston,_Texas_-_19690720.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>CAPCOM <a href="/wiki/Charles_Duke" title="Charles Duke">Charles Duke</a>, with backup crewmen <a href="/wiki/Jim_Lovell" title="Jim Lovell">Jim Lovell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fred_Haise" title="Fred Haise">Fred Haise</a> listening in during Apollo 11\'s descent</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capsule_communicator" title="Capsule communicator">capsule communicator</a> (CAPCOM) was an astronaut at the <a href="/wiki/Christopher_C._Kraft_Jr._Mission_Control_Center" title="Christopher C. Kraft Jr. Mission Control Center">Mission Control Center</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Houston,_Texas" title="Houston, Texas">Houston, Texas</a>, who was the only person who communicated directly with the flight crew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKranz200027_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKranz200027-44">[44]</a></sup> For Apollo 11, the CAPCOMs were: <a href="/wiki/Charles_Duke" title="Charles Duke">Charles Duke</a>, Ronald Evans, <a href="/wiki/Bruce_McCandless_II" title="Bruce McCandless II">Bruce McCandless II</a>, James Lovell, William Anders, Ken Mattingly, Fred Haise, <a href="/wiki/Don_L._Lind" title="Don L. Lind">Don L. Lind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Owen_K._Garriott" title="Owen K. Garriott">Owen K. Garriott</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harrison_Schmitt" title="Harrison Schmitt">Harrison Schmitt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979375_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979375-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight_directors">Flight directors</span></h3>\n<p>The four shift <a href="/wiki/Flight_controller#FLIGHT" title="Flight controller">flight directors</a> for this mission were:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000272_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000272-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKranz2000230,_236,_273,_320_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKranz2000230,_236,_273,_320-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Clifford_E._Charlesworth" title="Clifford E. Charlesworth">Clifford E. Charlesworth</a> (Green team), launch and <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_activity" title="Extravehicular activity">extravehicular activity</a> (EVA)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gerald_D._Griffin" title="Gerald D. Griffin">Gerald D. Griffin</a> (Gold team)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gene_Kranz" title="Gene Kranz">Gene Kranz</a> (White team), lunar landing</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glynn_Lunney" title="Glynn Lunney">Glynn Lunney</a> (Black team), lunar ascent</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparations">Preparations</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insignia">Insignia</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_insignia.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5432" data-file-width="5380" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Apollo_11_insignia.png/220px-Apollo_11_insignia.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Apollo_11_insignia.png/330px-Apollo_11_insignia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Apollo_11_insignia.png/440px-Apollo_11_insignia.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_insignia.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo 11 insignia</div></div></div>\n<p>The Apollo 11 <a href="/wiki/Mission_patch" title="Mission patch">mission emblem</a> was designed by Collins, who wanted a symbol for "peaceful lunar landing by the United States". At Lovell\'s suggestion, he chose the <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagle</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_bird" title="National bird">national bird</a> of the United States, as the symbol. Tom Wilson, a simulator instructor, suggested that they put an <a href="/wiki/Olive_branch" title="Olive branch">olive branch</a> in its beak to represent their peaceful mission. Collins added a lunar background with the Earth in the distance. The sunlight in the image was coming from the wrong direction; the shadow should have been in the lower part of the Earth instead of the left. Aldrin, Armstrong and Collins decided that the Eagle and the Moon would be in their natural colours, and decided on a blue and gold border. Armstrong was concerned that "eleven" would not be understood by non-English speakers, so they went with "Apollo 11",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001332&#8211;334_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001332&#8211;334-47">[47]</a></sup> and they decided not to put their names on the patch, so it would "be representative of <i>everyone</i> who had worked toward a lunar landing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001332_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001332-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>An illustrator at the MSC did the artwork, which was then sent off to NASA officials for approval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001332&#8211;334_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001332&#8211;334-47">[47]</a></sup> The design was rejected. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bob_Gilruth" title="Bob Gilruth">Bob Gilruth</a>, the director of the MSC felt that the talons of the eagle looked "too warlike".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001333_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001333-49">[49]</a></sup> After some discussion, the olive branch was moved to the talons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001333_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001333-49">[49]</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_dollar" title="Eisenhower dollar">Eisenhower dollar coin</a> was released in 1971, the patch design provided the eagle for its reverse side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The design was also used for the smaller <a href="/wiki/Susan_B._Anthony_dollar" title="Susan B. Anthony dollar">Susan B. Anthony dollar</a> unveiled in 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Call_signs">Call signs</span></h3>\n<p>After the crew of Apollo 10 named their spacecraft <i>Charlie Brown</i> and <i>Snoopy</i>, assistant manager for public affairs <a href="/wiki/Julian_Scheer" title="Julian Scheer">Julian Scheer</a> wrote to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_M._Low" title="George M. Low">George M. Low</a>, the  Manager of the Apollo Spacecraft Program Office at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manned_Spacecraft_Center" title="Manned Spacecraft Center">Manned Spacecraft Center</a> (MSC), to suggest the Apollo 11 crew be less flippant in naming their craft. The name <i>Snowcone</i> was used for the CM and <i>Haystack</i> was used for the LM in both internal and external communications during early mission planning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall_Space_Flight_Center19698_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall_Space_Flight_Center19698-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The LM was named <i>Eagle</i> after the motif which was featured prominently on the mission insignia. At Scheer\'s suggestion, the CM was named <i>Columbia</i> after  <i><a href="/wiki/Columbiad#In_fiction" title="Columbiad">Columbiad</a></i>, the giant cannon that launched a spacecraft (also from Florida) in <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">Jules Verne</a>\'s 1865 novel <i><a href="/wiki/From_the_Earth_to_the_Moon" title="From the Earth to the Moon">From the Earth to the Moon</a></i>. It also referenced <a href="/wiki/Columbia_(name)" title="Columbia (name)">Columbia</a>, a historical name of the United States. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001334&#8211;335_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001334&#8211;335-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979331_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979331-54">[54]</a></sup> In Collins\' 1976 book, he said <i>Columbia</i> was in reference to <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins1994116_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins1994116-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mementos">Mementos</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_(SN-416).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1851" data-file-width="3654" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Apollo_11_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_%28SN-416%29.jpg/220px-Apollo_11_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_%28SN-416%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Apollo_11_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_%28SN-416%29.jpg/330px-Apollo_11_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_%28SN-416%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Apollo_11_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_%28SN-416%29.jpg/440px-Apollo_11_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_%28SN-416%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Flown_Silver_Robbins_Medallion_(SN-416).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo 11 space-flown silver <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NASA_space-flown_Robbins_medallions_of_the_Apollo_missions" title="NASA space-flown Robbins medallions of the Apollo missions">Robbins medallion</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The astronaut had personal preference kits (PPKs), small bags containing personal items of significance that they wanted to take with them on the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Five 0.5-pound (0.23&#160;kg) PPKs were carried on Apollo 11: three (one for each astronaut) were stowed on <i>Columbia</i> before launch, and two on <i>Eagle</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Neil Armstrong\'s LM PPK contained a piece of wood from the <a href="/wiki/Wright_brothers" title="Wright brothers">Wright brothers</a>\' 1903 <i><a href="/wiki/Wright_Flyer" title="Wright Flyer">Wright Flyer</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s left propeller and a piece of fabric from its wing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2005527_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2005527-58">[58]</a></sup> along with a diamond-studded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronaut_pin" title="Astronaut pin">astronaut pin</a> originally given to <a href="/wiki/Deke_Slayton" title="Deke Slayton">Deke Slayton</a> by the widows of the Apollo 1 crew. This pin had been intended to be flown on that mission and given to Slayton afterwards; but following the disastrous launch pad fire and subsequent funerals, the widows gave the pin to Slayton. Armstrong took it with him on Apollo 11.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESlaytonCassutt1994191&#8211;192_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESlaytonCassutt1994191&#8211;192-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Site_selection">Site selection</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_site_selection_globe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="406" data-file-width="507" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Lunar_site_selection_globe.jpg/220px-Lunar_site_selection_globe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Lunar_site_selection_globe.jpg/330px-Lunar_site_selection_globe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Lunar_site_selection_globe.jpg/440px-Lunar_site_selection_globe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_site_selection_globe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Moon showing prospective sites for Apollo 11. Site 2 was chosen.</div></div></div>\n<p>NASA\'s Apollo Site Selection Board announced five potential landing sites on February 8, 1968. These were the result of two years\' worth of studies based on high-resolution photography of the lunar surface by the five unmanned probes of the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Orbiter_program" title="Lunar Orbiter program">Lunar Orbiter program</a> and information about surface conditions provided by the <a href="/wiki/Surveyor_program" title="Surveyor program">Surveyor program</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Site_Selection_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Site_Selection-60">[60]</a></sup> The best Earth-bound telescopes could not resolve features with the resolution Project Apollo required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECortright197579_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECortright197579-61">[61]</a></sup> The landing site had to be close to the lunar equator to minimize the amount of propellant required; clear of obstacles to minimize maneuvering, and flat to simplify the task of the landing radar. Scientific value was not a consideration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarland199919_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarland199919-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Areas that appeared promising on photographs taken on Earth were often found to be totally unacceptable. The original requirement that the site be free of craters had to be relaxed, as no such site was found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECortright197598&#8211;99_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECortright197598&#8211;99-63">[63]</a></sup> Five sites were considered: Sites 1 and 2 were in the Sea of Tranquility (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mare_Tranquilitatis" title="Mare Tranquilitatis">Mare Tranquilitatis</a></i>); Site 3 was in the Central Bay (<i><a href="/wiki/Sinus_Medii" title="Sinus Medii">Sinus Medii</a></i>); and Sites 4 and 5 were in the Ocean of Storms (<i><a href="/wiki/Oceanus_Procellarum" title="Oceanus Procellarum">Oceanus Procellarum</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Site_Selection_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Site_Selection-60">[60]</a></sup>\nThe final site selection was based on seven criteria:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The site needed to be smooth, with relatively few craters;</li>\n<li>with approach paths free of large hills, tall cliffs or deep craters that might confuse the landing radar and cause it to issue incorrect readings;</li>\n<li>reachable with a minimum amount of propellant;</li>\n<li>allowing for delays in the launch countdown;</li>\n<li>providing the Apollo spacecraft with a free-return trajectory, one that would allow it to coast around the Moon and safely return to Earth without requiring any engine firings should a problem arise on the way to the Moon;</li>\n<li>with good visibility during the landing approach, meaning that the Sun would be between 7 and 20 degrees behind the LM; and</li>\n<li>a general slope of less than 2 degrees in the landing area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Site_Selection_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-Site_Selection-60">[60]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>The requirement for the Sun angle was particularly restrictive, limiting the launch date to one day per month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Site_Selection_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-Site_Selection-60">[60]</a></sup> A landing just after dawn was chosen to limit the temperature extremes the astronauts would experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins19947_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins19947-64">[64]</a></sup> The Apollo Site Selection Board selected Site 2, with Sites 3 and 5 as backups in the event of the launch being delayed. In May 1969, Apollo 10\'s lunar module flew to within 15 kilometers (9.3&#160;mi) of Site 2, and reported that it was acceptable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECappellari1972976_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECappellari1972976-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_step_decision">First step decision</span></h3>\n<p>During the first press conference after the Apollo 11 crew was announced, the first question a reporter asked was, "Which one of you gentlemen will be the first man to step onto the lunar surface?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994148_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994148-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2005360_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2005360-68">[68]</a></sup> Slayton told the reporter that it had not been decided, and Armstrong added that it was "not based on individual desire".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994148_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994148-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of the first versions of the egress checklist had the lunar module pilot exit the spacecraft before the command module pilot, which matched what had been done in previous missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001347_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001347-69">[69]</a></sup> The commander had never performed the spacewalk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAldrinAbraham201657&#8211;58_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAldrinAbraham201657&#8211;58-70">[70]</a></sup> Reporters wrote in early 1969 that Aldrin would be the first to walk on the Moon, and Associate Administrator <a href="/wiki/George_Mueller_(NASA)" title="George Mueller (NASA)">George Mueller</a> told reporters he would be the first as well. Aldrin heard that Armstrong would be the first to step on the Moon because Armstrong was a civilian, which made Aldrin livid. Aldrin attempted to persuade other lunar module pilots he should be first, but they responded cynically about what they perceived as a lobbying campaign. Attempting to stem interdepartmental conflict, Slayton told Aldrin that Armstrong would be first since he was the commander. The decision was announced in a press conference on April 14, 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2005363&#8211;365_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2005363&#8211;365-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For decades, Aldrin believed the final decision was largely driven by the lunar module\'s hatch location. Because the astronauts had their spacesuits on and the spacecraft was so small, maneuvering to exit the spacecraft was difficult. The crew tried a simulation in which Aldrin left the spacecraft first, but he damaged the simulator while attempting to egress. While this was enough for mission planners to make their decision, Aldrin and Armstrong were left in the dark on the decision until late spring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994149_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994149-72">[72]</a></sup> Slayton told Armstrong the plan was to have him leave the spacecraft first, if he agreed. Armstrong said, "Yes, that&#8217;s the way to do it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994150_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994150-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The media accused Armstrong of exercising his commander\'s prerogative to exit the spacecraft first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchefter1999281_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchefter1999281-74">[74]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chris_Kraft" title="Chris Kraft">Chris Kraft</a> revealed in his 2001 autobiography that a meeting occurred between Gilruth, Slayton, Low, and himself to make sure Aldrin would not be the first to walk on the Moon. They argued that the first person to walk on the Moon should be like Charles Lindbergh, a calm and quiet person. They made the decision to change the flight plan so the commander was the first to egress from the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2005371&#8211;372_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2005371&#8211;372-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-launch">Pre-launch</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:69-HC-620_-_SA506.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="917" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/69-HC-620_-_SA506.jpg/220px-69-HC-620_-_SA506.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/69-HC-620_-_SA506.jpg/330px-69-HC-620_-_SA506.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/69-HC-620_-_SA506.jpg/440px-69-HC-620_-_SA506.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:69-HC-620_-_SA506.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Saturn V SA-506, the rocket carrying the Apollo 11 spacecraft, moves out of the <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_Assembly_Building" title="Vehicle Assembly Building">Vehicle Assembly Building</a> towards <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Launch_Complex_39" title="Launch Complex 39">Launch Complex 39</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The ascent stage of lunar module LM-5 arrived at the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a> on January 8, 1969, followed by the descent stage four days later, and Command and Service Module CM-107 on January 23.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mission_Overview_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mission_Overview-76">[76]</a></sup> There were several differences between LM-5 and Apollo 10\'s LM-4; LM-5 had a VHF radio antenna to facilitate communication with the astronauts during their EVA on the lunar surface; a lighter ascent engine; more thermal protection on the landing gear; and a package of scientific experiments known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Apollo_Scientific_Experiments_Package" title="Early Apollo Scientific Experiments Package">Early Apollo Scientific Experiments Package</a> (EASEP). The only change in the configuration of the command module was the removal of some insulation from the forward hatch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978472_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978472-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>  The command and service modules were mated on January 29, and moved from the <a href="/wiki/Operations_and_Checkout_Building" title="Operations and Checkout Building">Operations and Checkout Building</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_Assembly_Building" title="Vehicle Assembly Building">Vehicle Assembly Building</a> on April 14.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mission_Overview_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mission_Overview-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The  <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a> third stage of Saturn V AS-506 had arrived on January 18, followed by the <a href="/wiki/S-II" title="S-II">S-II</a> second stage on February 6, <a href="/wiki/S-IC" title="S-IC">S-IC</a> first stage on February 20, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn_V_Instrument_Unit" title="Saturn V Instrument Unit">Saturn V Instrument Unit</a> on February 27. At 1230 on May 20, the 5,443-tonne (5,357-long-ton; 6,000-short-ton) assembly departed the Vehicle Assembly Building atop the <a href="/wiki/Crawler-transporter" title="Crawler-transporter">crawler-transporter</a>, bound for Launch Pad 39A, part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Launch_Complex_39" title="Launch Complex 39">Launch Complex 39</a>, while Apollo 10 was still on its way to the Moon. A countdown test commenced on June 26, and concluded on July 2. The launch complex was floodlit on the night of July 15, when the crawler-transporter carried the <a href="/wiki/Service_structure" title="Service structure">mobile service structure</a> back to its parking area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mission_Overview_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mission_Overview-76">[76]</a></sup> In the early hours of the morning, the fuel tanks of the S-II and S-IVB stages were filled with <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978474_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978474-79">[79]</a></sup> Fueling was completed by three hours before launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978475_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978475-80">[80]</a></sup> Launch operations were partly automated, with 43 programs written in the <a href="/wiki/ATOLL_(programming_language)" title="ATOLL (programming language)">ATOLL programming language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978355&#8211;356_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978355&#8211;356-81">[81]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Slayton roused the crew shortly after 0400, and they showered, shaved, and had the traditional pre-flight breakfast of steak and eggs with Slayton and the backup crew. They then donned their space suits and began breathing pure oxygen. At 0630, they headed out to Launch Complex 39.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001355&#8211;357_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001355&#8211;357-82">[82]</a></sup> Haise entered <i>Columbia</i> about three hours and ten minutes before launch time. Along with a technician, he helped Armstrong into the left hand couch at 06:54. Five minutes later, Collins joined him, taking up his position on the right hand couch. Finally, Aldrin entered, taking the center couch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978475_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978475-80">[80]</a></sup> Haise left around two hours and ten minutes before launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The closeout crew sealed the hatch, and the cabin was purged and pressurized. The closeout crew then left the launch complex about an hour before launch time. The countdown became automated at three minutes and twenty seconds before launch time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978475_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978475-80">[80]</a></sup> Over 450 personnel were at the consoles in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firing_room" title="Firing room">firing room</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978474_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978474-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission">Mission</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_and_flight_to_lunar_orbit">Launch and flight to lunar orbit</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Saturn_V_lifting_off_on_July_16,_1969.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2862" data-file-width="2280" decoding="async" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Apollo_11_Saturn_V_lifting_off_on_July_16%2C_1969.jpg/220px-Apollo_11_Saturn_V_lifting_off_on_July_16%2C_1969.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Apollo_11_Saturn_V_lifting_off_on_July_16%2C_1969.jpg/330px-Apollo_11_Saturn_V_lifting_off_on_July_16%2C_1969.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Apollo_11_Saturn_V_lifting_off_on_July_16%2C_1969.jpg/440px-Apollo_11_Saturn_V_lifting_off_on_July_16%2C_1969.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Saturn_V_lifting_off_on_July_16,_1969.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Apollo 11 Saturn V space vehicle lifts off with Astronauts Neil A. Armstrong, Michael Collins and Edwin E. Aldrin Jr. at 9:32 a.m. EDT July 16, 1969, from Kennedy Space Center\'s Launch Complex 39A.</div></div></div>\n<p>An estimated one million spectators watched the launch of Apollo 11 from the highways and beaches in the vicinity of the launch site. Dignitaries included the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Staff_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Chief of Staff of the United States Army">Chief of Staff of the United States Army</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_(United_States)" title="General (United States)">General</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Westmoreland" title="William Westmoreland">William Westmoreland</a>, four <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_States" title="Cabinet of the United States">cabinet members</a>, 19 <a href="/wiki/Governor_(United_States)" title="Governor (United States)">state governors</a>, 40 <a href="/wiki/Mayoralty_in_the_United_States" title="Mayoralty in the United States">mayors</a>, 60 <a href="/wiki/Ambassador" title="Ambassador">ambassadors</a> and 200 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congressmen" title="Congressmen">congressmen</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President</a> <a href="/wiki/Spiro_Agnew" title="Spiro Agnew">Spiro Agnew</a> viewed the launch with the former president, <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Lady_Bird_Johnson" title="Lady Bird Johnson">Lady Bird Johnson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978474_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978474-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBilstein1980369&#8211;370_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBilstein1980369&#8211;370-84">[84]</a></sup> Around 3,500 media representatives were present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979338_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979338-85">[85]</a></sup> About two-thirds were from the United States; the rest came from 55 other countries. The launch was televised live in 33 countries, with an estimated 25 million viewers in the United States alone. Millions more around the world listened to radio broadcasts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBilstein1980369&#8211;370_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBilstein1980369&#8211;370-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978474_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBensonFaherty1978474-79">[79]</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> viewed the launch from his office in the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> with his NASA liaison officer, Apollo astronaut <a href="/wiki/Frank_Borman" title="Frank Borman">Frank Borman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Saturn V AS-506 launched Apollo 11 on July 16, 1969, at 13:32:00 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a> (9:32:00 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Daylight_Time" title="Eastern Daylight Time">EDT</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>  It entered Earth orbit at an altitude of 100.4 nautical miles (185.9&#160;km) by 98.9 nautical miles (183.2&#160;km), twelve minutes later. After one and a half orbits, the S-IVB third-stage engine pushed the spacecraft onto its trajectory toward the Moon with the <a href="/wiki/Trans-lunar_injection" title="Trans-lunar injection">trans-lunar injection</a> (TLI) burn at 16:22:13 UTC. About 30 minutes later, with Collins in the left seat and at the controls, the <a href="/wiki/Transposition,_docking,_and_extraction" title="Transposition, docking, and extraction">transposition, docking, and extraction</a> maneuver was performed. This involved separating <i>Columbia</i> from the spent S-IVB stage, turning around, and docking with <i>Eagle</i> still attached to the stage. After the LM was extracted, the combined spacecraft headed for the Moon, while the rocket stage flew on a trajectory past the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001374&#8211;375_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001374&#8211;375-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000106_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000106-2">[2]</a></sup> This was done to avoid colliding with the spacecraft, the Earth, or the Moon. A <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">slingshot effect</a> from passing around the Moon threw it into an <a href="/wiki/Heliocentric_orbit" title="Heliocentric orbit">orbit around the Sun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall_Space_Flight_Center19697_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall_Space_Flight_Center19697-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On July 19 at 17:21:50 UTC, Apollo 11 passed behind the Moon and fired its service propulsion engine to enter <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit" title="Lunar orbit">lunar orbit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000106_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000106-2">[2]</a></sup> In the thirty orbits that followed, the crew saw passing views of their landing site in the southern Sea of Tranquillity about 12 miles (19&#160;km) southwest of the crater <a href="/wiki/Collins_(crater)" title="Collins (crater)">Sabine D</a>. The site was selected in part because it had been characterized as relatively flat and smooth by the automated <a href="/wiki/Ranger_8" title="Ranger 8">Ranger 8</a> and <a href="/wiki/Surveyor_5" title="Surveyor 5">Surveyor 5</a> landers and the Lunar Orbiter mapping spacecraft and unlikely to present major landing or EVA challenges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> It lay about 25 kilometers (16&#160;mi) southeast of the Surveyor 5 landing site, and 68 kilometers (42&#160;mi) southwest of Ranger 8\'s crash site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center1969130_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center1969130-91">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_descent">Lunar descent</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_CSM_photographed_from_Lunar_Module_(AS11-37-5445).jpg"><img alt="The top of the silvery command module is seen over a grey, cratered lunar surface" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4112" data-file-width="4112" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Apollo_11_CSM_photographed_from_Lunar_Module_%28AS11-37-5445%29.jpg/220px-Apollo_11_CSM_photographed_from_Lunar_Module_%28AS11-37-5445%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Apollo_11_CSM_photographed_from_Lunar_Module_%28AS11-37-5445%29.jpg/330px-Apollo_11_CSM_photographed_from_Lunar_Module_%28AS11-37-5445%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Apollo_11_CSM_photographed_from_Lunar_Module_%28AS11-37-5445%29.jpg/440px-Apollo_11_CSM_photographed_from_Lunar_Module_%28AS11-37-5445%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_CSM_photographed_from_Lunar_Module_(AS11-37-5445).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Columbia</i> in lunar orbit, photographed from <i>Eagle</i></div></div></div>\n<p>At 12:52:00 UTC on July 20, Aldrin and Armstrong entered <i>Eagle</i>, and began the final preparations for lunar descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000106_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000106-2">[2]</a></sup> At 17:44:00 <i>Eagle</i> separated from <i>Columbia</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000107_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000107-6">[6]</a></sup> Collins, alone aboard <i>Columbia</i>, inspected <i>Eagle</i> as it pirouetted before him to ensure the craft was not damaged, and that the landing gear was correctly deployed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center19699_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center19699-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollinsAldrin1975209_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollinsAldrin1975209-93">[93]</a></sup> Armstrong exclaimed: "The <i>Eagle</i> has wings!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollinsAldrin1975209_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollinsAldrin1975209-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As the descent began, Armstrong and Aldrin found that they were passing landmarks on the surface two or three seconds early, and reported that they were "long"; they would land miles west of their target point. <i>Eagle</i> was traveling too fast. The problem could have been <a href="/wiki/Mass_concentration_(astronomy)" title="Mass concentration (astronomy)">mascons</a>&#8212;concentrations of high mass that could have altered the trajectory. Flight Director Gene Kranz speculated that it could have resulted from extra air pressure in the docking tunnel. Or it could have been the result of <i>Eagle</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s pirouette maneuver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMindell2008220&#8211;221_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMindell2008220&#8211;221-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center196982_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center196982-95">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Five minutes into the descent burn, and 6,000 feet (1,800&#160;m) above the surface of the Moon, the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Guidance_Computer" title="Apollo Guidance Computer">LM guidance computer</a> (LGC) distracted the crew with the first of several unexpected 1201 and 1202 program alarms. Inside Mission Control Center, computer engineer <a href="/wiki/Jack_Garman" title="Jack Garman">Jack Garman</a> told <a href="/wiki/Flight_controller#GUIDO" title="Flight controller">Guidance Officer</a> <a href="/wiki/Steve_Bales" title="Steve Bales">Steve Bales</a> it was safe to continue the descent, and this was relayed to the crew. The program alarms indicated "executive overflows", meaning the guidance computer could not complete all of its tasks in real time and had to postpone some of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollinsAldrin1975210&#8211;212_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollinsAldrin1975210&#8211;212-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamiltonHackler200834&#8211;43_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamiltonHackler200834&#8211;43-97">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Hamilton_(scientist)" title="Margaret Hamilton (scientist)">Margaret Hamilton</a>, the Director of Apollo Flight Computer Programming at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT">MIT</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Stark_Draper_Laboratory" title="Charles Stark Draper Laboratory">Charles Stark Draper Laboratory</a> later recalled:\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Lunar_Module_Eagle_in_landing_configuration_in_lunar_orbit_from_the_Command_and_Service_Module_Columbia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2229" data-file-width="2480" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Apollo_11_Lunar_Module_Eagle_in_landing_configuration_in_lunar_orbit_from_the_Command_and_Service_Module_Columbia.jpg/220px-Apollo_11_Lunar_Module_Eagle_in_landing_configuration_in_lunar_orbit_from_the_Command_and_Service_Module_Columbia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Apollo_11_Lunar_Module_Eagle_in_landing_configuration_in_lunar_orbit_from_the_Command_and_Service_Module_Columbia.jpg/330px-Apollo_11_Lunar_Module_Eagle_in_landing_configuration_in_lunar_orbit_from_the_Command_and_Service_Module_Columbia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Apollo_11_Lunar_Module_Eagle_in_landing_configuration_in_lunar_orbit_from_the_Command_and_Service_Module_Columbia.jpg/440px-Apollo_11_Lunar_Module_Eagle_in_landing_configuration_in_lunar_orbit_from_the_Command_and_Service_Module_Columbia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Lunar_Module_Eagle_in_landing_configuration_in_lunar_orbit_from_the_Command_and_Service_Module_Columbia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Eagle</i> in <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit" title="Lunar orbit">lunar orbit</a> photographed from <i>Columbia</i></div></div></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>To blame the computer for the Apollo 11 problems is like blaming the person who spots a fire and calls the fire department. Actually, the computer was programmed to do more than recognize error conditions. A complete set of recovery programs was incorporated into the software. The software\'s action, in this case, was to eliminate lower priority tasks and re-establish the more important ones. The computer, rather than almost forcing an abort, prevented an abort. If the computer hadn\'t recognized this problem and taken recovery action, I doubt if Apollo 11 would have been the successful Moon landing it was.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>During the mission, the cause was diagnosed as the rendezvous radar switch being in the wrong position, causing the computer to process data from both the rendezvous and landing radars at the same time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center1969190&#8211;192_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center1969190&#8211;192-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Software engineer Don Eyles concluded in a 2005 Guidance and Control Conference paper that the problem was due to a hardware design bug previously seen during testing of the first unmanned LM in <a href="/wiki/Apollo_5" title="Apollo 5">Apollo 5</a>. Having the rendezvous radar on (so that it was warmed up in case of an emergency landing abort) should have been irrelevant to the computer, but an electrical phasing mismatch between two parts of the rendezvous radar system could cause the stationary antenna to appear to the computer as dithering back and forth between two positions, depending upon how the hardware randomly powered up. The extra spurious <a href="/wiki/Cycle_stealing" title="Cycle stealing">cycle stealing</a>, as the rendezvous radar updated an involuntary counter, caused the computer alarms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Landing">Landing</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Landing_Site_%26_West_Crater.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="1400" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Apollo_11_Landing_Site_%26_West_Crater.png/220px-Apollo_11_Landing_Site_%26_West_Crater.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Apollo_11_Landing_Site_%26_West_Crater.png/330px-Apollo_11_Landing_Site_%26_West_Crater.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Apollo_11_Landing_Site_%26_West_Crater.png/440px-Apollo_11_Landing_Site_%26_West_Crater.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Landing_Site_%26_West_Crater.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Landing site relative to West crater</div></div></div>\n<p>When Armstrong again looked outside, he saw that the computer\'s landing target was in a boulder-strewn area just north and east of a 300-foot (91&#160;m) diameter crater (later determined to be <a href="/wiki/West_(lunar_crater)" title="West (lunar crater)">West crater</a>), so he took semi-automatic control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994196_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994196-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMindell2008195&#8211;197_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMindell2008195&#8211;197-103">[103]</a></sup> Armstrong considered landing short of the boulder field so they could collect geological samples from it, but could not since their horizontal velocity was too fast. Throughout the descent, Aldrin called out navigation data to Armstrong, who was busy piloting <i>Eagle</i>. Now 107 feet (33&#160;m) above the surface, Armstrong knew their propellant supply was dwindling and was determined to land at the first possible landing site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994197_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994197-104">[104]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Armstrong found a clear patch of ground and maneuvered the spacecraft towards it. As he got closer, now 250 feet (76&#160;m) above the surface, he discovered his new landing site had a crater in it. He cleared the crater and found another patch of level ground. They were now 100 feet (30&#160;m) from the surface, with only 90 seconds of propellant remaining. Lunar dust kicked up by the LM\'s engines began to obfuscate his ability to determine the spacecraft\'s motion. Some large rocks jutted out of the dust cloud, and Armstrong focused on them during his descent so he could determine the spacecraft\'s speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994198&#8211;199_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994198&#8211;199-105">[105]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A few moments before the landing, a light informed Aldrin that at least one of the 67-inch (170&#160;cm) probes hanging from <i>Eagle</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> footpads had touched the surface, and he said: "Contact light!" Armstrong was supposed to immediately shutdown the engines, as the engineers suspected the pressure caused by the engines own exhaust reflecting off the lunar surface could make them explode, but forgot. Three seconds later, <i>Eagle</i> landed and Armstrong shutdown the engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994199_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994199-106">[106]</a></sup> Aldrin immediately said "Okay, engine stop. ACA &#8211; out of <a href="/wiki/Detent" title="Detent">detent</a>." Armstrong acknowledged: "Out of detent. Auto." Aldrin continued: "Mode control &#8211; both auto. Descent engine command override off. Engine arm &#8211; off. 413 is in."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMindell2008226_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMindell2008226-107">[107]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:320px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/AP11_FINAL_APPROACH.ogv/320px--AP11_FINAL_APPROACH.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:320px;height:240px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="270" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="AP11_FINAL_APPROACH.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/1/19/AP11_FINAL_APPROACH.ogv/AP11_FINAL_APPROACH.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm;
