class="selfreference"><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>While still on the ladder, Armstrong uncovered a <a href="/wiki/Lunar_plaque" title="Lunar plaque">plaque</a> mounted on the LM descent stage bearing two drawings of Earth (of the Western and Eastern Hemispheres), an inscription, and signatures of the astronauts and President Nixon. The inscription read:\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Here men from the planet Earth first set foot upon the Moon, July 1969 A.D. We came in peace for all mankind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALSJ_4_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-ALSJ_4-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>After describing the surface dust as "very fine-grained" and "almost like a powder",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALSJ_4_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-ALSJ_4-7">[7]</a></sup> at 02:56:15, six and a half hours after landing, Armstrong stepped off <i>Eagle</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> footpad and declared: "That\'s one small step for [a] man, one giant leap for mankind."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000108_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000108-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Armstrong intended to say "That\'s one small step for a man", but the word "a" is not audible in the transmission, and thus was not initially reported by most observers of the live broadcast. When later asked about his quote, Armstrong said he believed he said "for a man", and subsequent printed versions of the quote included the "a" in square brackets. One explanation for the absence may be that his accent caused him to slur the words "for a" together; another is the intermittent nature of the audio and video links to Earth, partly because of storms near Parkes Observatory. More recent digital analysis of the tape claims to reveal the "a" may have been spoken but obscured by static.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>\n</p><p>About seven minutes after stepping onto the Moon\'s surface, Armstrong collected a contingency soil sample using a sample bag on a stick. He then folded the bag and tucked it into a pocket on his right thigh. This was to guarantee there would be some lunar soil brought back in case an emergency required the astronauts to abandon the EVA and return to the LM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Twelve minutes after the sample was collected,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000108_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000108-120">[120]</a></sup> he removed the TV camera from the MESA and made a panoramic sweep, then mounted it on a tripod.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALSJ_3_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-ALSJ_3-113">[113]</a></sup> The TV camera cable remained partly coiled and presented a tripping hazard throughout the EVA. Still photography was accomplished with a <a href="/wiki/Hasselblad" title="Hasselblad">Hasselblad</a> camera which could be operated hand held or mounted on Armstrong\'s <a href="/wiki/Apollo/Skylab_A7L" title="Apollo/Skylab A7L">Apollo/Skylab A7L</a> space suit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center196923_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center196923-126">[126]</a></sup> Aldrin joined Armstrong on the surface. He described the view with the simple phrase: "Magnificent desolation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALSJ_4_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-ALSJ_4-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Armstrong said that moving in the <a href="/wiki/Gravitation_of_the_Moon" title="Gravitation of the Moon">lunar gravity</a>, one-sixth of Earth\'s, was "even perhaps easier than the simulations&#160;... It\'s absolutely no trouble to walk around."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALSJ_4_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-ALSJ_4-7">[7]</a></sup> Aldrin joined him on the surface and tested methods for moving around, including two-footed kangaroo hops. The PLSS backpack created a tendency to tip backward, but neither astronaut had serious problems maintaining balance. Loping became the preferred method of movement. The astronauts reported that they needed to plan their movements six or seven steps ahead. The fine soil was quite slippery. Aldrin remarked that moving from sunlight into <i>Eagle</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> shadow produced no temperature change inside the suit, but the helmet was warmer in sunlight, so he felt cooler in shadow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALSJ_4_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-ALSJ_4-7">[7]</a></sup> The MESA failed to provide a stable work platform and was in shadow, slowing work somewhat. As they worked, the moonwalkers kicked up gray dust which soiled the outer part of their suits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center196923_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center196923-126">[126]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3967" data-file-width="3929" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg/220px-Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg/330px-Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg/440px-Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aldrin salutes the deployed United States flag on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_surface" title="Lunar surface">lunar surface</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The astronauts planted the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Flag_Assembly" title="Lunar Flag Assembly">Lunar Flag Assembly</a> containing a <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_the_United_States" title="Flag of the United States">flag of the United States</a> on the lunar surface, in clear view of the TV camera. Aldrin remembered, "Of all the jobs I had to do on the Moon the one I wanted to go the smoothest was the flag raising."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theattic_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-theattic-127">[127]</a></sup> But the astronauts struggled with the telescoping rod and could only jam the pole a couple of inches (5&#160;cm) into the hard lunar surface. Aldrin was afraid it might topple in front of TV viewers. But he gave "a crisp West Point salute".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theattic_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-theattic-127">[127]</a></sup> Before Aldrin could take a photo of Armstrong with the flag, President Richard Nixon spoke to them through a telephone-radio transmission which Nixon called "the most historic phone call ever made from the White House."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Nixon originally had a long speech prepared to read during the phone call, but Frank Borman, who was at the White House as a NASA liaison during Apollo 11, convinced Nixon to keep his words brief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBormanSerling1988237&#8211;238_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBormanSerling1988237&#8211;238-129">[129]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><b>Nixon:</b> Hello, Neil and Buzz. I\'m talking to you by telephone from the Oval Room at the White House. And this certainly has to be the most historic telephone call ever made. I just can\'t tell you how proud we all are of what you\'ve done. For every American, this has to be the proudest day of our lives. And for people all over the world, I am sure they too join with Americans in recognizing what an immense feat this is. Because of what you have done, the heavens have become a part of man\'s world. And as you talk to us from the Sea of Tranquillity, it inspires us to redouble our efforts to bring peace and tranquillity to Earth. For one priceless moment in the whole history of man, all the people on this Earth are truly one: one in their pride in what you have done, and one in our prayers that you will return safely to Earth.\n</p><p><b>Armstrong:</b> Thank you, Mr. President. It\'s a great honor and privilege for us to be here, representing not only the United States, but men of peace of all nations, and with interest and curiosity, and men with a vision for the future. It\'s an honor for us to be able to participate here today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_bootprint.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2363" data-file-width="2349" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Apollo_11_bootprint.jpg/220px-Apollo_11_bootprint.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Apollo_11_bootprint.jpg/330px-Apollo_11_bootprint.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Apollo_11_bootprint.jpg/440px-Apollo_11_bootprint.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_bootprint.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aldrin\'s bootprint; part of an experiment to test the properties of the lunar <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">regolith</a></div></div></div>\n<p>They deployed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Apollo_Scientific_Experiments_Package" title="Early Apollo Scientific Experiments Package">EASEP</a>, which included a passive seismic experiment package used to measure <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moonquake" title="Moonquake">moonquakes</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Retroreflector#Retroreflectors_on_the_Moon" title="Retroreflector">retroreflector</a> array used for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_laser_ranging_experiment" title="Lunar laser ranging experiment">lunar laser ranging experiment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EASEP_Deployment_and_Closeout_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-EASEP_Deployment_and_Closeout-131">[131]</a></sup> Then Armstrong walked 196 feet (60&#160;m) from the LM to snap photos at the rim of Little West Crater  while Aldrin collected two <a href="/wiki/Core_sample" title="Core sample">core samples</a>. He used the <a href="/wiki/Geologist%27s_hammer" title="Geologist\'s hammer">geologist\'s hammer</a> to pound in the tubes &#8211; the only time the hammer was used on Apollo 11, but was unable to penetrate more than 6 inches (15&#160;cm) deep. The astronauts then collected rock samples using scoops and tongs on extension handles. Many of the surface activities took longer than expected, so they had to stop documenting sample collection halfway through the allotted 34 minutes. Aldrin shoveled 6 kilograms (13&#160;lb) of soil into the box of rocks in order to pack them in tightly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarland199928&#8211;29_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarland199928&#8211;29-132">[132]</a></sup> Two types of rocks were found in the geological samples: <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Breccia" title="Breccia">breccia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Three new minerals were discovered in the rock samples collected by the astronauts: <a href="/wiki/Armalcolite" title="Armalcolite">armalcolite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tranquillityite" title="Tranquillityite">tranquillityite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pyroxferroite" title="Pyroxferroite">pyroxferroite</a>. Armalcolite was named after Armstrong, Aldrin, and Collins. All have subsequently been found on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Mission Control used a coded phrase to warn Armstrong that his metabolic rates were high, and that he should slow down. He was moving rapidly from task to task as time ran out. As metabolic rates remained generally lower than expected for both astronauts throughout the walk, Mission Control granted the astronauts a 15-minute extension.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EASEP_Deployment_and_Closeout_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-EASEP_Deployment_and_Closeout-131">[131]</a></sup> In a 2010 interview, Armstrong explained that NASA limited the first moonwalk\'s time and distance because there was no empirical proof of how much cooling water the astronauts\' PLSS backpacks would consume to handle their body heat generation while working on the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_ascent">Lunar ascent</span></h3>\n<p>Aldrin entered <i>Eagle</i> first. With some difficulty the astronauts lifted film and two sample boxes containing 21.55 kilograms (47.5&#160;lb) of lunar surface material to the LM hatch using a flat cable pulley device called the Lunar Equipment Conveyor (LEC). This proved to be an inefficient tool, and later missions preferred to carry equipment and samples up to the LM by hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALSJ_3_113-3"><a href="#cite_note-ALSJ_3-113">[113]</a></sup> Armstrong reminded Aldrin of a bag of memorial items in his sleeve pocket, and Aldrin tossed the bag down. Armstrong then jumped onto the ladder\'s third rung, and climbed into the LM. After transferring to LM <a href="/wiki/Life_support" title="Life support">life support</a>, the explorers lightened the ascent stage for the return to lunar orbit by tossing out their PLSS backpacks, lunar overshoes, an empty Hasselblad camera, and other equipment. The hatch was closed again at 05:01. They then pressurized the LM and settled down to sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALSJ_6_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-ALSJ_6-136">[136]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aldrin_Looks_Back_at_Tranquility_Base_-_GPN-2000-001102.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3904" data-file-width="3904" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Aldrin_Looks_Back_at_Tranquility_Base_-_GPN-2000-001102.jpg/220px-Aldrin_Looks_Back_at_Tranquility_Base_-_GPN-2000-001102.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Aldrin_Looks_Back_at_Tranquility_Base_-_GPN-2000-001102.jpg/330px-Aldrin_Looks_Back_at_Tranquility_Base_-_GPN-2000-001102.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Aldrin_Looks_Back_at_Tranquility_Base_-_GPN-2000-001102.jpg/440px-Aldrin_Looks_Back_at_Tranquility_Base_-_GPN-2000-001102.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aldrin_Looks_Back_at_Tranquility_Base_-_GPN-2000-001102.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aldrin next to the Passive Seismic Experiment Package with <i>Eagle</i> in the background</div></div></div>\n<p>Nixon\'s speech writer <a href="/wiki/William_Safire" title="William Safire">William Safire</a> had prepared <i>In Event of Moon Disaster</i> for the President to read on television in the event the Apollo 11 astronauts were stranded on the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> The contingency plan originated in a memo from Safire to Nixon\'s <a href="/wiki/White_House_Chief_of_Staff" title="White House Chief of Staff">White House Chief of Staff</a> <a href="/wiki/H._R._Haldeman" title="H. R. Haldeman">H. R. Haldeman</a>, in which Safire suggested a protocol the administration might follow in reaction to such a disaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-safire_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-safire-139">[139]</a></sup> According to the plan, Mission Control would "close down communications" with the LM, and a clergyman would "commend their souls to the deepest of the deep" in a public ritual likened to <a href="/wiki/Burial_at_sea" title="Burial at sea">burial at sea</a>. The last line of the prepared text contained an allusion to <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Brooke" title="Rupert Brooke">Rupert Brooke</a>\'s First World War poem, "<a href="/wiki/The_Soldier_(poem)" title="The Soldier (poem)">The Soldier</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-safire_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-safire-139">[139]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While moving inside the cabin, Aldrin accidentally damaged the <a href="/wiki/Circuit_breaker" title="Circuit breaker">circuit breaker</a> that would arm the main engine for lift off from the Moon. There was a concern this would prevent firing the engine, stranding them on the Moon. A felt-tip pen was sufficient to activate the switch; had this not worked, the LM circuitry could have been reconfigured to allow firing the ascent engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALSJ_6_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-ALSJ_6-136">[136]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After more than &#8203;<span class="frac nowrap">21<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span> hours on the lunar surface, in addition to the scientific instruments, the astronauts left behind an Apollo 1 mission patch and a memorial bag containing a gold replica of an olive branch as a traditional symbol of peace and a silicon message disk. The disk carries the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11_goodwill_messages" title="Apollo 11 goodwill messages">goodwill statements</a> by Presidents Eisenhower, Kennedy, Johnson, and Nixon and messages from leaders of 73 countries around the world. The disk also carries a listing of the leadership of the US Congress, a listing of members of the four committees of the House and Senate responsible for the NASA legislation, and the names of NASA\'s past and present top management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_photo_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="709" data-file-width="709" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Apollo_11_photo_map.svg/220px-Apollo_11_photo_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Apollo_11_photo_map.svg/330px-Apollo_11_photo_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Apollo_11_photo_map.svg/440px-Apollo_11_photo_map.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_photo_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing landing site and photos taken</div></div></div>\n<p>After about seven hours of rest, the crew was awakened by Houston to prepare for the return flight. Two and a half hours later, at 17:54:00 UTC, they lifted off in <i>Eagle</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> ascent stage to rejoin Collins aboard <i>Columbia</i> in lunar orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000108_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000108-120">[120]</a></sup> Film taken from the LM ascent stage upon liftoff from the Moon reveals the American flag, planted some 25 feet (8&#160;m) from the descent stage, whipping violently in the exhaust of the ascent stage engine. Aldrin looked up in time to witness the flag topple: "The ascent stage of the LM separated&#160;... I was concentrating on the computers, and Neil was studying the <a href="/wiki/Attitude_indicator" title="Attitude indicator">attitude indicator</a>, but I looked up long enough to see the flag fall over."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollinsAldrin1975219_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollinsAldrin1975219-141">[141]</a></sup> Subsequent Apollo missions usually planted the American flags further from the LM to prevent them being blown over by the ascent engine exhaust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Columbia_in_lunar_orbit"><i>Columbia</i> in lunar orbit</span></h3>\n<p>During his day flying solo around the Moon, Collins never felt lonely. Although it has been said "not since <a href="/wiki/Adam" title="Adam">Adam</a> has any human known such solitude",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001402_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001402-143">[143]</a></sup> Collins felt very much a part of the mission. In his autobiography he wrote: "this venture has been structured for three men, and I consider my third to be as necessary as either of the other two".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001402_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001402-143">[143]</a></sup> In the 48 minutes of each orbit when he was out of radio contact with the Earth while <i>Columbia</i> passed round the far side of the Moon, the feeling he reported was not fear or loneliness, but rather "awareness, anticipation, satisfaction, confidence, almost exultation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001402_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001402-143">[143]</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of Collins\' first tasks was to identify the lunar module on the ground. To give Collins an idea where to look, Mission Control radioed that they believed the lunar module landed about four miles off target. Each time he passed over the suspected lunar landing site, he tried in vain to find the module. On his first orbits on the back side of the Moon, Collins performed maintenance activities such as dumping excess water produced by the <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">fuel cells</a> and preparing the cabin for Armstrong and Aldrin to return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001401&#8211;407_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001401&#8211;407-144">[144]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Just before he reached the dark side on the third orbit, Mission Control informed Collins that there was a problem with the temperature of the coolant. If it became too cold, parts of <i>Columbia</i> might freeze. Mission Control advised him to assume manual control and implement Environmental Control System Malfunction Procedure 17. Instead, Collins flicked the switch on the offending system from automatic to manual and back to automatic again, and carried on with normal housekeeping chores, while keeping an eye on the temperature. When <i>Columbia</i> came back around to the near side of the Moon again, he was able to report that the problem had been resolved. For the next couple of orbits, he described his time on the back side of the Moon as "relaxing". After Aldrin and Armstrong completed their EVA, Collins slept so he could be rested for the rendezvous. While the flight plan called for <i>Eagle</i> to meet up with <i>Columbia</i>, Collins was prepared for certain contingencies in which he would fly <i>Columbia</i> down to meet <i>Eagle</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2001406&#8211;408,_410_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2001406&#8211;408,_410-145">[145]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return">Return</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_lunar_module.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2306" data-file-width="2389" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Apollo_11_lunar_module.jpg/220px-Apollo_11_lunar_module.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Apollo_11_lunar_module.jpg/330px-Apollo_11_lunar_module.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Apollo_11_lunar_module.jpg/440px-Apollo_11_lunar_module.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_lunar_module.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Eagle</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> ascent stage approaching <i>Columbia</i></div></div></div>\n<p><i>Eagle</i> rendezvoused with <i>Columbia</i> at 21:24 UTC on July 21, and the two docked at 21:35. <i>Eagle</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s ascent stage was jettisoned into lunar orbit at 23:41.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000109_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000109-3">[3]</a></sup> Just before the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_12" title="Apollo 12">Apollo 12</a> flight, it was noted that <i>Eagle</i> was still likely to be orbiting the Moon. Later NASA reports mentioned that <i>Eagle</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> orbit had decayed, resulting in it impacting in an "uncertain location" on the lunar surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nOn July 23, the last night before splashdown, the three astronauts made a television broadcast in which Collins commented: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>...&#160;The Saturn V rocket which put us in orbit is an incredibly complicated piece of machinery, every piece of which worked flawlessly&#160;... We have always had confidence that this equipment will work properly. All this is possible only through the blood, sweat, and tears of a number of people&#160;... All you see is the three of us, but beneath the surface are thousands and thousands of others, and to all of those, I would like to say, "Thank you very much."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollinsAldrin1975222_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollinsAldrin1975222-147">[147]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote><p>\nAldrin added: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>This has been far more than three men on a mission to the Moon; more, still, than the efforts of a government and industry team; more, even, than the efforts of one nation. We feel that this stands as a symbol of the insatiable curiosity of all mankind to explore the unknown&#160;... Personally, in reflecting on the events of the past several days, a verse from Psalms comes to mind. "When I consider the heavens, the work of Thy fingers, the Moon and the stars, which Thou hast ordained; What is man that Thou art mindful of him?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollinsAldrin1975222_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollinsAldrin1975222-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote><p>\nArmstrong concluded: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The responsibility for this flight lies first with history and with the giants of science who have preceded this effort; next with the American people, who have, through their will, indicated their desire; next with four administrations and their Congresses, for implementing that will; and then, with the agency and industry teams that built our spacecraft, the Saturn, the Columbia, the Eagle, and the little <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_Mobility_Unit" title="Extravehicular Mobility Unit">EMU</a>, the spacesuit and backpack that was our small spacecraft out on the lunar surface. We would like to give special thanks to all those Americans who built the spacecraft; who did the construction, design, the tests, and put their hearts and all their abilities into those craft. To those people tonight, we give a special thank you, and to all the other people that are listening and watching tonight, God bless you. Good night from Apollo 11."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollinsAldrin1975222_147-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollinsAldrin1975222-147">[147]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>On the return to Earth, a bearing at the Guam tracking station failed, potentially preventing communication on the last segment of the Earth return. A regular repair was not possible in the available time but the station director, Charles Force, had his ten-year-old son Greg use his small hands to reach into the housing and pack it with grease. Greg was later thanked by Armstrong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Splashdown_and_quarantine">Splashdown and quarantine</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Splashdown_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1131" data-file-width="1179" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Splashdown_3.jpg/220px-Splashdown_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Splashdown_3.jpg/330px-Splashdown_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Splashdown_3.jpg/440px-Splashdown_3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Splashdown_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Columbia</i> floats on the ocean as Navy divers assist in retrieving the astronauts</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carrier</a> <a href="/wiki/USS_Hornet_(CV-12)" title="USS Hornet (CV-12)">USS&#160;<i>Hornet</i></a>, under the command of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Captain_(United_States_Navy)" title="Captain (United States Navy)">Captain</a> <a href="/wiki/Carl_J._Seiberlich" title="Carl J. Seiberlich">Carl J. Seiberlich</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmichael20103_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmichael20103-150">[150]</a></sup> was selected as the primary recovery ship (PRS) for Apollo 11 on June 5, replacing its sister ship, the <a href="/wiki/Landing_Platform_Helicopter" title="Landing Platform Helicopter">LPH</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USS_Princeton_(LPH-5)" title="USS Princeton (LPH-5)">USS&#160;<i>Princeton</i></a>, which had recovered Apollo 10 on May 26. <i>Hornet</i> was then at her home port of <a href="/wiki/Long_Beach,_California" title="Long Beach, California">Long Beach, California</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmichael201021_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmichael201021-151">[151]</a></sup> On reaching <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Harbor" title="Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor</a> on July 5, <i>Hornet</i> embarked the <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_SH-3_Sea_King" title="Sikorsky SH-3 Sea King">Sikorsky SH-3 Sea King</a> helicopters of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HS-4" title="HS-4">HS-4</a>, a unit which specialized in recovery of Apollo spacecraft, specialized divers of <a href="/wiki/Underwater_Demolition_Team" title="Underwater Demolition Team">UDT</a> Detachment Apollo, a 35-man NASA recovery team, and about 120 media representatives. To make room, most of <i>Hornet</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s air wing was left behind in Long Beach. Special recovery equipment was also loaded, including a <a href="/wiki/Boilerplate_(spaceflight)" title="Boilerplate (spaceflight)">boilerplate</a> command module used for training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmichael201038&#8211;43,_71&#8211;72_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmichael201038&#8211;43,_71&#8211;72-152">[152]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On July 12, with Apollo 11 still on the launch pad, <i>Hornet</i> departed Pearl Harbor for the recovery area in the central Pacific,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmichael201085_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmichael201085-153">[153]</a></sup> in the vicinity of <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Apollo_11&amp;params=10_36_N_172_24_E_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">10&#176;36&#8242;N</span> <span class="longitude">172&#176;24&#8242;E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">&#65279; / &#65279;</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">10.600&#176;N 172.400&#176;E</span><span style="display:none">&#65279; / <span class="geo">10.600; 172.400</span></span></span></a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> A presidential party consisting of Nixon, Borman, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> <a href="/wiki/William_P._Rogers" title="William P. Rogers">William P. Rogers</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Advisor_(United_States)" title="National Security Advisor (United States)">National Security Advisor</a> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a> flew to <a href="/wiki/Johnston_Atoll" title="Johnston Atoll">Johnston Atoll</a> on <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_One" title="Air Force One">Air Force One</a>, then to the <a href="/wiki/Command_ship" title="Command ship">command ship</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USS_Arlington_(AGMR-2)" title="USS Arlington (AGMR-2)">USS&#160;<i>Arlington</i></a> in  <a href="/wiki/Marine_One" title="Marine One">Marine One</a>. After a night on board, they would fly to <i>Hornet</i> in Marine One for a few hours of ceremonies. On arrival aboard <i>Hornet</i>, the party was greeted by the  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Pacific_Command" title="United States Pacific Command">Commander-in-Chief, Pacific Command (CINCPAC)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Admiral_(United_States)" title="Admiral (United States)">Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/John_S._McCain_Jr." title="John S. McCain Jr.">John S. McCain Jr.</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NASA_Administrator" title="NASA Administrator">NASA Administrator</a> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_O._Paine" title="Thomas O. Paine">Thomas O. Paine</a>, who flew to <i>Hornet</i> from <a href="/wiki/Pago_Pago" title="Pago Pago">Pago Pago</a> in one of <i>Hornet</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s <a href="/wiki/Carrier_onboard_delivery" title="Carrier onboard delivery">carrier onboard delivery</a> aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmichael2010107&#8211;108,_145&#8211;146_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmichael2010107&#8211;108,_145&#8211;146-155">[155]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Weather satellites were not yet common, but US Air Force <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Captain_(O-3)" title="Captain (O-3)">Captain</a> Hank Brandli had access to top secret spy satellite images. He realized that a storm front was headed for the Apollo recovery area. Poor visibility was a serious threat to the mission; if the helicopters could not locate <i>Columbia</i>, the spacecraft, its crew, and its priceless cargo of Moon rocks might be lost. Brandli alerted Navy Captain Willard S. Houston Jr., the commander of the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Typhoon_Warning_Center" title="Joint Typhoon Warning Center">Fleet Weather Center</a> at Pearl Harbor, who had the required security clearance. On their recommendation, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rear_Admiral_(United_States)" title="Rear Admiral (United States)">Rear Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/Donald_C._Davis" title="Donald C. Davis">Donald C. Davis</a>, the commander of Manned Spaceflight Recovery Forces, Pacific, advised NASA to change the recovery area. This was done; a new one was designated,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmichael2010136&#8211;137,_144&#8211;145_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmichael2010136&#8211;137,_144&#8211;145-156">[156]</a></sup> 215 nautical miles (398&#160;km) northeast of the original.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALSJ_Re-entry_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-ALSJ_Re-entry-157">[157]</a></sup>\n</p><p>This altered the flight plan. A different sequence of computer programs was used, one never before attempted. In a conventional entry, P64 was followed by P67. For a skip-out re-entry, P65 and P66 were employed to handle the exit and entry parts of the skip. In this case, because they were extending the re-entry but not actually skipping out, P66 was not invoked and instead P65 led directly to P67. The crew were also warned that they would not be in a full-lift (heads-down) attitude when they entered P67.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALSJ_Re-entry_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-ALSJ_Re-entry-157">[157]</a></sup> The first program\'s acceleration subjected the astronauts to 6.5 standard gravities (64&#160;m/s<sup>2</sup>); the second, to 6.0 standard gravities (59&#160;m/s<sup>2</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center196928_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center196928-158">[158]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Before dawn on July 24, <i>Hornet</i> launched four Sea King helicopters and three <a href="/wiki/Grumman_E-1_Tracer" title="Grumman E-1 Tracer">Grumman E-1 Tracers</a>. Two of the E-1s were designated as "air boss" while the third acted as a communications relay aircraft. Two of the Sea Kings carried divers and recovery equipment. The third carried photographic equipment, and the fourth carried the decontamination swimmer and the flight surgeon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center1969169&#8211;170_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center1969169&#8211;170-159">[159]</a></sup> At 16:44 UTC (05:44 local time) <i>Columbia</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s <a href="/wiki/Drogue_parachute" title="Drogue parachute">drogue parachutes</a> were deployed. This was observed by the helicopters. Seven minutes later <i>Columbia</i> struck the water forcefully 2,660&#160;km (1,440&#160;nmi) east of <a href="/wiki/Wake_Island" title="Wake Island">Wake Island</a>, 380&#160;km (210&#160;nmi) south of Johnston Atoll, and 24&#160;km (13&#160;nmi) from <i>Hornet</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000109_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrloff2000109-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALSJ_Re-entry_157-2"><a href="#cite_note-ALSJ_Re-entry-157">[157]</a></sup> at <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Apollo_11&amp;params=13_19_N_169_9_W_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">13&#176;19&#8242;N</span> <span class="longitude">169&#176;9&#8242;W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">&#65279; / &#65279;</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">13.317&#176;N 169.150&#176;W</span><span style="display:none">&#65279; / <span class="geo">13.317; -169.150</span></span></span></a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center1969170_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center1969170-160">[160]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/Splashdown" title="Splashdown">splashdown</a>, <i>Columbia</i> landed upside down but was righted within ten minutes by flotation bags activated by the astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center1969164&#8211;167_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center1969164&#8211;167-161">[161]</a></sup> A diver from the Navy helicopter hovering above attached a <a href="/wiki/Sea_anchor" title="Sea anchor">sea anchor</a> to prevent it from drifting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmichael2010184&#8211;185_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmichael2010184&#8211;185-162">[162]</a></sup> More divers attached flotation collars to stabilize the module and positioned rafts for astronaut extraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmichael2010186&#8211;188_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmichael2010186&#8211;188-163">[163]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:President_Nixon_welcomes_the_Apollo_11_astronauts_aboard_the_U.S.S._Hornet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6862" data-file-width="6862" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/President_Nixon_welcomes_the_Apollo_11_astronauts_aboard_the_U.S.S._Hornet.jpg/220px-President_Nixon_welcomes_the_Apollo_11_astronauts_aboard_the_U.S.S._Hornet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/President_Nixon_welcomes_the_Apollo_11_astronauts_aboard_the_U.S.S._Hornet.jpg/330px-President_Nixon_welcomes_the_Apollo_11_astronauts_aboard_the_U.S.S._Hornet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/President_Nixon_welcomes_the_Apollo_11_astronauts_aboard_the_U.S.S._Hornet.jpg/440px-President_Nixon_welcomes_the_Apollo_11_astronauts_aboard_the_U.S.S._Hornet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:President_Nixon_welcomes_the_Apollo_11_astronauts_aboard_the_U.S.S._Hornet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The crew of Apollo 11 in <a href="/wiki/Quarantine" title="Quarantine">quarantine</a> after returning to Earth, visited by Richard Nixon</div></div></div>\n<p>The divers then passed biological isolation garments (BIGs) to the astronauts, and assisted them into the life raft. The possibility of bringing back <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a> from the lunar surface was considered remote, but NASA took precautions at the recovery site. The astronauts were rubbed down with a <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hypochlorite" title="Sodium hypochlorite">sodium hypochlorite</a> solution and <i>Columbia</i> wiped with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Betadine" title="Betadine">Betadine</a> to remove any lunar dust that might be present. The astronauts were winched on board the recovery helicopter. BIGs were worn until they reached isolation facilities on board <i>Hornet</i>. The raft containing decontamination materials was intentionally sunk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center1969164&#8211;167_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center1969164&#8211;167-161">[161]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After touchdown on <i>Hornet</i> at 17:53 UTC, the helicopter was lowered by the elevator into the hangar bay, where the astronauts walked the 30 feet (9.1&#160;m) to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mobile_Quarantine_Facility" title="Mobile Quarantine Facility">Mobile Quarantine Facility</a> (MQF), where they would begin the Earth-based portion of their 21 days of quarantine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmichael2010199&#8211;200_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmichael2010199&#8211;200-164">[164]</a></sup> This practice would continue for two more Apollo missions, Apollo 12 and <a href="/wiki/Apollo_14" title="Apollo 14">Apollo 14</a>, before the Moon was proven to be barren of life, and the quarantine process dropped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> Nixon welcomed the astronauts back to Earth. He told them: "As a result of what you\'ve done, the world has never been closer together before."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After Nixon departed, <i>Hornet</i> was brought alongside the 5-short-ton (4.5&#160;t) <i>Columbia</i>, which was lifted aboard by the ship\'s crane, placed on a <a href="/wiki/Dolly_(trailer)" title="Dolly (trailer)">dolly</a> and moved next to the MQF. It was then attached to the MQF with a flexible tunnel, allowing the lunar samples, film, data tapes and other items to be removed. <i>Hornet</i> returned to Pearl Harbor, where the MQF was loaded onto a <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_C-141_Starlifter" title="Lockheed C-141 Starlifter">Lockheed C-141 Starlifter</a> and airlifted to the Manned Spacecraft Center. The astronauts arrived at the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Receiving_Laboratory" title="Lunar Receiving Laboratory">Lunar Receiving Laboratory</a> at 10:00 UTC on July 28. <i>Columbia</i> was taken to <a href="/wiki/Ford_Island" title="Ford Island">Ford Island</a> for deactivation, and its pyrotechnics made safe. It was then taken to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hickham_Air_Force_Base" title="Hickham Air Force Base">Hickham Air Force Base</a>, from whence it was flown to Houston in a <a href="/wiki/Douglas_C-133_Cargomaster" title="Douglas C-133 Cargomaster">Douglas C-133 Cargomaster</a>, reaching the Lunar Receiving Laboratory on July 30.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center1969166,_171&#8211;173_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManned_Spacecraft_Center1969166,_171&#8211;173-167">[167]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In accordance with the <a href="/wiki/Extra-Terrestrial_Exposure_Law" title="Extra-Terrestrial Exposure Law">Extra-Terrestrial Exposure Law</a>, a set of regulations promulgated by NASA on July 16 to codify its quarantine protocol,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> the astronauts continued in quarantine. After three weeks in confinement (first in the Apollo spacecraft, then in their trailer on <i>Hornet</i>, and finally in the Lunar Receiving Laboratory), the astronauts were given a clean bill of health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> On August 10, 1969, the Interagency Committee on Back Contamination met in Atlanta and lifted the quarantine on the astronauts, on those who had joined them in quarantine (NASA physician <a href="/wiki/William_Carpentier" title="William Carpentier">William Carpentier</a> and MQF project engineer <a href="/wiki/John_Hirasaki" title="John Hirasaki">John Hirasaki</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmichael2010118_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmichael2010118-170">[170]</a></sup> and on <i>Columbia</i> itself. Loose equipment from the spacecraft remained in isolation until the lunar samples were released for study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEErtelNewkirkBrooks1978312_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEErtelNewkirkBrooks1978312-171">[171]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Celebrations">Celebrations</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_ticker_tape_parade_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1997" data-file-width="2842" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Apollo_11_ticker_tape_parade_1.jpg/220px-Apollo_11_ticker_tape_parade_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Apollo_11_ticker_tape_parade_1.jpg/330px-Apollo_11_ticker_tape_parade_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Apollo_11_ticker_tape_parade_1.jpg/440px-Apollo_11_ticker_tape_parade_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_ticker_tape_parade_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ticker tape parade in New York City</div></div></div>\n<p>On August 13, the three astronauts rode in ticker-tape parades in their honor in New York and Chicago, with an estimated six million attendees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LADinner_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-LADinner-172">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> On the same evening in Los Angeles there was an official <a href="/wiki/State_dinner" title="State dinner">state dinner</a> to celebrate the flight, attended by members of Congress, 44 governors, the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice of the United States</a>, and ambassadors from 83 nations at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Century_Plaza_Hotel" title="Century Plaza Hotel">Century Plaza Hotel</a>. Nixon and Agnew honored each astronaut with a presentation of the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LADinner_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-LADinner-172">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The three astronauts spoke before a <a href="/wiki/Joint_session_of_the_United_States_Congress" title="Joint session of the United States Congress">joint session of Congress</a> on September 16, 1969. They presented two US flags, one to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> and the other to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a>, that had been carried to the surface of the Moon with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_American_Samoa" title="Flag of American Samoa">flag of American Samoa</a> on Apollo 11 is on display at the <a href="/wiki/Jean_P._Haydon_Museum" title="Jean P. Haydon Museum">Jean P. Haydon Museum</a> in Pago Pago, the capital of American Samoa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>\n</p><p>This celebration was the beginning of a 38-day world tour that brought the astronauts to 22 foreign countries and included visits with the leaders of many countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apollo_11_Crew_Starts_World_Tour_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Apollo_11_Crew_Starts_World_Tour-177">[177]</a></sup> The crew toured from September 29 to November 5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apollo_11_Crew_Starts_World_Tour_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-Apollo_11_Crew_Starts_World_Tour-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> Many nations honored the first human <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing" title="Moon landing">Moon landing</a> with special features in magazines or by issuing Apollo 11 commemorative postage stamps or coins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_significance">Cultural significance</span></h3>\n<p>Humans walking on the Moon and returning safely to Earth accomplished Kennedy\'s goal set eight years earlier. In Mission Control during the Apollo 11 landing, Kennedy\'s speech flashed on the screen, followed by the words "TASK ACCOMPLISHED, July 1969".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Launius_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Launius-181">[181]</a></sup> The success of Apollo 11 demonstrated the United States\' technological superiority over other nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Launius_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-Launius-181">[181]</a></sup> With the success of Apollo 11, America had won the Space Race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaikin200757_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaikin200757-182">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchefter1999288_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchefter1999288-183">[183]</a></sup>\n</p><p>New phrases permeated into the English language. "If they can send a man to the Moon, why can\'t they..." became a common saying following Apollo 11.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> Armstrong\'s famous words on the lunar surface also spun off countless parodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaikin200757_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaikin200757-182">[182]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While most celebrated the accomplishment, disenfranchised Americans saw it as a symbol of the divide in America, evidenced by protesters outside of Kennedy Space Center the day before Apollo 11 launched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchefter1999283_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchefter1999283-185">[185]</a></sup> This is not to say that they were not awed by it. <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Abernathy" title="Ralph Abernathy">Ralph Abernathy</a>, leading a protest march, was so captivated by the spectacle of the Apollo 11 launch that he forgot what he was going to say.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979338_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksGrimwoodSwenson1979338-85">[85]</a></sup> Racial and financial inequalities frustrated citizens who wondered why money spent on the Apollo program was not spent taking care of humans on Earth. A poem by <a href="/wiki/Gil_Scott-Heron" title="Gil Scott-Heron">Gil Scott-Heron</a> called "Whitey on the Moon" illustrated the <a href="/wiki/Racial_inequality_in_the_United_States" title="Racial inequality in the United States">racial inequality in the United States</a> that was highlighted by the Space Race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaikin200757_182-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaikin200757-182">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whitey_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-whitey-187">[187]</a></sup> The poem starts with:\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">\n<p>A rat done bit my sister Nell.<br/>\n(with Whitey on the moon)<br/>\nHer face and arms began to swell.<br/>\n(and Whitey&#8217;s on the moon)<br/>\nI can&#8217;t pay no doctor bill.<br/>\n(but Whitey&#8217;s on the moon)<br/>\nTen years from now I&#8217;ll be paying still.<br/>\n(while Whitey&#8217;s on the moon)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whitey_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-whitey-187">[187]</a></sup>\n</p>\n</div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Twenty percent of the world\'s population watched humans walk on the Moon for the first time. While Apollo 11 sparked the interest of the world, the follow-on Apollo missions did not hold the interest of the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Launius_181-2"><a href="#cite_note-Launius-181">[181]</a></sup> One possible explanation was the shift in complexity. Landing someone on the Moon was an easy goal to understand; lunar geology was too abstract for the average person. Another is that Kennedy\'s goal of landing humans on the Moon had already been accomplished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaikin200758_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaikin200758-188">[188]</a></sup> A well-defined objective helped Project Apollo accomplish its goal, but after it was completed it was hard to justify continuing the lunar missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While most Americans were proud of their nation\'s achievements in space exploration, only once during the late 1960s did the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallup_Poll" title="Gallup Poll">Gallup Poll</a> indicate that a majority of Americans favored "doing more" in space as opposed to "doing less". By 1973, 59 percent of those polled favored cutting spending on space exploration. The Space Race had ended, and Cold War tensions were easing as the US and Soviet Union entered the era of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A9tente" title="D&#233;tente">d&#233;tente</a>. This was also a time when <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a> was rising, which put pressure on the government to reduce spending. What saved the space program was that it was one of the few government programs that had achieved something great. Drastic cuts, warned <a href="/wiki/Caspar_Weinberger" title="Caspar Weinberger">Caspar Weinberger</a>, the deputy director of the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Management_and_Budget" title="Office of Management and Budget">Office of Management and Budget</a>, might send a signal that "our best years are behind us".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCurdy1997106&#8211;107_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCurdy1997106&#8211;107-191">[191]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the Apollo 11 mission, officials from the Soviet Union said that landing humans on the Moon was dangerous and unnecessary. At the time the Soviet Union was attempting to retrieve lunar samples robotically. The Soviets publicly denied there was a race to the Moon, and indicated that they were not making an attempt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994631_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaikin1994631-192">[192]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mstislav_Keldysh" title="Mstislav Keldysh">Mstislav Keldysh</a> said in July 1969 that, "We are concentrating wholly on the creation of large satellite systems". It was revealed in 1989 that the Soviets had tried to send people to the Moon, but were unable to due to technological difficulties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> The public\'s reaction in the Soviet Union was mixed. The Soviet government limited the release of information about the lunar landing, which affected the reaction. A portion of the populace did not give it any attention, and another portion was angered by it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft">Spacecraft</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Columbia.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="801" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Apollo_11_Columbia.png/220px-Apollo_11_Columbia.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Apollo_11_Columbia.png/330px-Apollo_11_Columbia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Apollo_11_Columbia.png/440px-Apollo_11_Columbia.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Columbia.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Columbia</i> on display in the Milestones of Flight exhibition hall at the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Command Module <i>Columbia</i> went on a tour of the United States, visiting 49 state capitals, the <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">District of Columbia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anchorage,_Alaska" title="Anchorage, Alaska">Anchorage, Alaska</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> In 1971, it was transferred to the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>, and was displayed at the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a> (NASM) in Washington, DC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> It was in the central <i>Milestones of Flight</i> exhibition hall in front of the Jefferson Drive entrance, sharing the main hall with other pioneering flight vehicles such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Wright_Flyer" title="Wright Flyer">Wright Flyer</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Spirit_of_St._Louis" title="Spirit of St. Louis">Spirit of St. Louis</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Bell_X-1" title="Bell X-1">Bell X-1</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_American_X-15" title="North American X-15">North American X-15</a> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friendship_7" title="Friendship 7">Friendship 7</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Columbia</i> was moved in 2017 to the NASM Mary Baker Engen Restoration Hangar at the <a href="/wiki/Steven_F._Udvar-Hazy_Center" title="Steven F. Udvar-Hazy Center">Steven F. Udvar-Hazy Center</a> in Chantilly, Virginia, to be readied for a four-city tour titled <i>Destination Moon: The Apollo 11 Mission</i>. This included <a href="/wiki/Space_Center_Houston" title="Space Center Houston">Space Center Houston</a> from October 14, 2017 to March 18, 2018, the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Louis_Science_Center" title="Saint Louis Science Center">Saint Louis Science Center</a> from April 14 to September 3, 2018, the Senator John <a href="/wiki/Heinz_History_Center" title="Heinz History Center">Heinz History Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a> from September 29, 2018 to February 18, 2019, and the <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a> <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Flight" title="Museum of Flight">Museum of Flight</a> from March 16 to September 2, 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For 40 years Armstrong\'s and Aldrin\'s space suits were displayed in the museum\'s <i>Apollo to the Moon</i> exhibit,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> until it closed for good on December 3, 2018, to be replaced by a new gallery which was scheduled to open in 2022. A special display of Armstrong\'s suit is planned for the 50th anniversary of Apollo 11 in July 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> The quarantine trailer, the flotation collar and the flotation bags are in the Smithsonian\'s <a href="/wiki/Steven_F._Udvar-Hazy_Center" title="Steven F. Udvar-Hazy Center">Steven F. Udvar-Hazy Center</a> annex near <a href="/wiki/Washington_Dulles_International_Airport" title="Washington Dulles International Airport">Washington Dulles International Airport</a> in Chantilly, Virginia, where they are on display along with a test lunar module.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Mobile_Quarantine_Facility_at_the_Steven_F_Udvar-Hazy_Center_in_2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2302" data-file-width="3067" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Apollo_11_Mobile_Quarantine_Facility_at_the_Steven_F_Udvar-Hazy_Center_in_2009.jpg/220px-Apollo_11_Mobile_Quarantine_Facility_at_the_Steven_F_Udvar-Hazy_Center_in_2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Apollo_11_Mobile_Quarantine_Facility_at_the_Steven_F_Udvar-Hazy_Center_in_2009.jpg/330px-Apollo_11_Mobile_Quarantine_Facility_at_the_Steven_F_Udvar-Hazy_Center_in_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Apollo_11_Mobile_Quarantine_Facility_at_the_Steven_F_Udvar-Hazy_Center_in_2009.jpg/440px-Apollo_11_Mobile_Quarantine_Facility_at_the_Steven_F_Udvar-Hazy_Center_in_2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Mobile_Quarantine_Facility_at_the_Steven_F_Udvar-Hazy_Center_in_2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Apollo 11 Mobile Quarantine Facility on display at the <a href="/wiki/Steven_F._Udvar-Hazy_Center" title="Steven F. Udvar-Hazy Center">Steven F. Udvar-Hazy Center</a> in 2009</div></div></div>\n<p>The descent stage of the LM <i>Eagle</i> remains on the Moon. In 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter">Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter</a> (LRO) imaged the various Apollo landing sites on the surface of the Moon, for the first time with sufficient resolution to see the descent stages of the lunar modules, scientific instruments, and foot trails made by the astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> The remains of the ascent stage lie at an unknown location on the lunar surface, after being abandoned and impacting the Moon. The location is uncertain because <i>Eagle</i> ascent stage was not tracked after it was jettisoned, and the lunar gravity field is sufficiently non-uniform to make the orbit of the spacecraft unpredictable after a short time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 2012 a team of specialists financed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon.com" title="Amazon.com">Amazon</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Bezos" title="Jeff Bezos">Jeff Bezos</a> located the <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_F-1" title="Rocketdyne F-1">F-1 engines</a> from the S-IC stage that launched Apollo 11 into space. They were found on the Atlantic seabed using advanced sonar scanning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> His team brought parts of two of the five engines to the surface. In July 2013, a conservator discovered a serial number under the rust on one of the engines raised from the Atlantic, which NASA confirmed was from Apollo 11.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup>\nThe S-IVB third stage which performed Apollo 11\'s trans-lunar injection remains in a solar orbit near to that of Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moon_rocks">Moon rocks</span></h3>\n<p>The main repository for the Apollo Moon rocks is the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Sample_Laboratory_Facility" title="Lunar Sample Laboratory Facility">Lunar Sample Laboratory Facility</a> at the Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Houston,_Texas" title="Houston, Texas">Houston, Texas</a>. For safekeeping, there is also a smaller collection stored at <a href="/wiki/White_Sands_Test_Facility" title="White Sands Test Facility">White Sands Test Facility</a> near <a href="/wiki/Las_Cruces,_New_Mexico" title="Las Cruces, New Mexico">Las Cruces, New Mexico</a>. Most of the rocks are stored in nitrogen to keep them free of moisture. They are handled only indirectly, using special tools. Over 100 research laboratories around the world conduct studies of the samples, and approximately 500 samples are prepared and sent to investigators every year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 1969, Nixon asked NASA  to make up about 250 presentation <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11_lunar_sample_display" title="Apollo 11 lunar sample display">Apollo 11 lunar sample displays</a> for 135 nations, the fifty states of the United States and its possessions, and the United Nations. Each display included Moon dust from Apollo 11. The rice-sized particles were four small pieces of Moon soil weighing about 50&#160;mg and were enveloped in a clear acrylic button about as big as a <a href="/wiki/Half_dollar_(United_States_coin)" title="Half dollar (United States coin)">United States half dollar coin</a>. This acrylic button magnified the grains of lunar dust. The Apollo 11 lunar sample displays were given out as goodwill gifts by Nixon in 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Passive Seismic Experiment ran until the command uplink failed on August 25, 1969. The downlink failed on December 14, 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBatesLauderdaleKernaghan19792-3,_4-32_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBatesLauderdaleKernaghan19792-3,_4-32-215">[215]</a></sup>  As of  2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Apollo_11&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Laser_Ranging_experiment" title="Lunar Laser Ranging experiment">Lunar Laser Ranging experiment</a> remains operational.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="40th_anniversary_events">40th anniversary events</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Command_Module_in_Hangar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Apollo_11_Command_Module_in_Hangar.jpg/220px-Apollo_11_Command_Module_in_Hangar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Apollo_11_Command_Module_in_Hangar.jpg/330px-Apollo_11_Command_Module_in_Hangar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Apollo_11_Command_Module_in_Hangar.jpg/440px-Apollo_11_Command_Module_in_Hangar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Command_Module_in_Hangar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Columbia</i> at the Mary Baker Engen Restoration Hangar</div></div></div>\n<p>On July 15, 2009, <a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life.com</a> released a photo gallery of previously unpublished photos of the astronauts taken by <i>Life</i> photographer <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Morse" title="Ralph Morse">Ralph Morse</a> prior to the Apollo 11 launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> From July 16 to 24, 2009, NASA streamed the original mission audio on its website in real time 40 years to the minute after the events occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> It is in the process of restoring the video footage and has released a preview of key moments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> In July 2010, air-to-ground voice recordings and film footage shot in Mission Control during the Apollo 11 powered descent and landing was re-synchronized and released for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_Presidential_Library_and_Museum" title="John F. Kennedy Presidential Library and Museum">John F. Kennedy Presidential Library and Museum</a> set up an <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Flash" title="Adobe Flash">Adobe Flash</a> website that rebroadcasts the transmissions of Apollo 11 from launch to landing on the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On July 20, 2009, Armstrong, Aldrin, and Collins met with U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> at the White House.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> "We expect that there is, as we speak, another generation of kids out there who are looking up at the sky and are going to be the next Armstrong, Collins, and Aldrin", Obama said. "We want to make sure that NASA is going to be there for them when they want to take their journey."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> On August 7, 2009, an act of Congress awarded the three astronauts a <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Gold_Medal" title="Congressional Gold Medal">Congressional Gold Medal</a>, the highest civilian award in the United States. The bill was sponsored by Florida Senator <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bill_Nelson_(politician)" title="Bill Nelson (politician)">Bill Nelson</a> and Florida Representative <a href="/wiki/Alan_Grayson" title="Alan Grayson">Alan Grayson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A group of British scientists interviewed as part of the anniversary events reflected on the significance of the Moon landing:\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It was carried out in a technically brilliant way with risks taken&#160;... that would be inconceivable in the risk-averse world of today&#160;... The Apollo programme is arguably the greatest technical achievement of mankind to date&#160;... nothing since Apollo has come close [to] the excitement that was generated by those astronauts &#8211; Armstrong, Aldrin and the 10 others who followed them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="50th_Anniversary_events">50th Anniversary events</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11_Fiftieth_Anniversary_commemorative_coins" title="Apollo 11 Fiftieth Anniversary commemorative coins">Apollo 11 Fiftieth Anniversary commemorative coins</a></div>\n<p>On June 10, 2015, United States Congressman <a href="/wiki/Bill_Posey" title="Bill Posey">Bill Posey</a> introduced resolution H.R. 2726 to the 114th session of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> directing the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Mint" title="United States Mint">United States Mint</a> to design and sell commemorative coins in Gold, Silver and Clad for the 50th Anniversary of the Apollo 11 mission. On January 24, 2019, the United States Mint released the coins to the public on their website.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n</div>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-orbit-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-orbit_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-orbit_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-orbit_1-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-orbit_1-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-orbit_1-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20130829082429/http://airandspace.si.edu/explore-and-learn/topics/apollo/as11/a11sum.htm" rel="nofollow">"Apollo 11 Mission Summary"</a>. <i>The Apollo Program</i>.
